                    <tr><td><hr class="player-page-info-sep"/><div class="player-page-rotowire"><strong><a href="https://www.rotowire.com/users/ad_jump.asp?id=688&playerid=11848">RotoWire News</a>:</strong> Escobar signed a one-year, $4.85 million deal with the Twins on Thursday to avoid arbitration, Bob Nightengale of USA Today reports. (1/11/2018)</div></td></tr>

    <tr>
        
        
        
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>The 23-year-old Escobar debuted late last year and could start 2012 as the White Sox’s utility infielder, a role for which he seems well-suited but ultimately resigned to. There’s not much bat (.666 OPS career) and only average speed, but he could be the beneficiary if Gordon Beckham fails to improve. His eligibility at multiple infield positions could make him a decent AL-only reserve. (Jason Catania)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Because of the vacuous cavern the Twins have had at shortstop since 2010, Escobar will have a decent shot to win the job in Spring Training. Unfortunately, for fantasy purposes, his upside is Brendan Ryan. Yes, that Brendan Ryan, the one with a .633 career OPS. (Dan Wade)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Acquired in the Francisco Liriano trade with the White Sox, Escobar has all the makings of the next Twins utility guy written all over him. Statistically, Escobar hasn't done much with the bat, at least not until at Triple-A in 2013. But he has good pop for someone his size (5'10", 175 lbs), and he can literally play anywhere on the diamond. The Twins brass hasn't slapped him with the utility label yet, but the writing is on the wall for the 24-year-old Venezuelan. And besides, there are worse things than being a long-term bench jockey getting 200-250 plate appearances each year. (Brandon Warne)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>As long as Escobar can continue to show he can handle shortstop, he should be in the mix for a bench job with the Twins for the next few years to come.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Escobar was a surprising piece in what amounted to a pretty good Twins offense in 2014. The pint-sized infielder hit .275/.315/.406, and stroked 35 doubles to rank behind only Jhonny Peralta among shortstops. Where it all came from is a bit of a head-scratcher; Escobar has shown good pop at times but has never really put it all together for an extended period. Prior to 2014, he had hit just .228/.280/.307 as a big leaguer (332 plate appearances), and he has still only hit .269/.316/.358 as a minor leaguer. It seems as though the Twins view him as a luxurious utility player, which is probably the best way to keep him from being overexposed. Danny Santana will likely play shortstop full-time in 2015, leaving Escobar to man third, short and second behind a trio of guys who could all regress moving forward. It's not ideal for Escobar, but it's a nice position for the Twins to be in. (Brandon Warne)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Even if Escobar secures the starting shortstop job again, it's unlikely he'll hit like he did last season (.336 batting average on balls in play).</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Escobar surprised for the second season in a row for the Twins, doing so for a different reason this time around. In 2014, Escobar hit .275 with 35 doubles, surprising some with that pop from the middle infield. In 2015, his batting average on balls in play declined from .336 to .301, but he made up for it by showing even more pop, stroking 12 home runs with 31 doubles. For a 27-year-old who didn't display much power at any level of the minors, that's a positive development, and he got even hotter late in the year when handed the starting reigns at short full time. Prior to that point, he managed to get in five or more games at four positions and 10 or more at three, so he'll be a flexible multi-position option in 2016, depending on your league's minimums. Is the power surge sustainable? Well, the switch hitter finally managed to hit righties regularly, improving his plate coverage from the left side, and he hits the ball close to league average hardness despite a smaller stature, pointing to a slow, multi-year power development. The Twins will probably give Danny Santana another shot to win the job in spring training, but if Escobar can lock it down, he could be a cheap source of 10-plus home runs without killing your batting average. (Blake Murphy)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Escobar continued a quiet power development by unexpectedly smacking 12 home runs in 2015, eight of them in the season's final two months. That's beyond what can be expected in 2016, but if he wins the starting shortstop gig again, his low-double-digits power and positional flexibility could make him a cheap flier in deeper formats.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Injuries sapped most of Escobar's productivity last year, as he hit just .236/.280/.338 after back-to-back years with a weighted on-base average over .315. Prior to 2016, Escobar had evolved into a solid shortstop/utility man, with limited discipline but fairly good contact skills and solid pop for his size. In 2016, Escobar missed time with hamstring and groin issues, and at times ceded playing time to Eduardo Nunez, and down the stretch, Jorge Polanco, as he was outplayed by both players offensively by a wide margin. Escobar's role is unclear heading into 2017; he could be the full-time shortstop in the event of a Brian Dozier trade, as that would move Polanco to second base and leave the team without an obvious bridge to Nick Gordon, who is probably two years away. But if the team holds on to Dozier, Escobar might be scraping for at-bats behind him, Polanco and Miguel Sano at third base. He's not a great fantasy consideration as a starter; as a utility player, he's persona non grata. He's a great clubhouse guy and a good teammate — he's affectionately dubbed Dozier "O'Conner" due to his resemblance to Paul Walker's "Fast and the Furious" character — but unless he's a full-time starting shortstop, he'll have no value in fantasy leagues. (@Brandon_Warne)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>If Escobar's named the starting shortstop for the Twins, he could provide an owner with a cheap source of doubles, a few home runs and a so-so batting average. That'll only happen if Dozier is traded, and if the team doesn't make a subsequent move to fill shortstop behind that. Don't hold your breath.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
</table>
